### Cabinet and inauguration

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Cabinet and inauguration")\]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/President_Garfield_in_reviewing_stand%2C_viewing_inauguration_ceremonies%2C_March_4%2C_1881_LCCN00650941.tif/lossy-page1-200px-President_Garfield_in_reviewing_stand%2C_viewing_inauguration_ceremonies%2C_March_4%2C_1881_LCCN00650941.tif.jpg)](/wiki/File:President%5FGarfield%5Fin%5Freviewing%5Fstand,%5Fviewing%5Finauguration%5Fceremonies,%5FMarch%5F4,%5F1881%5FLCCN00650941.tif)

President Garfield in reviewing stand, viewing inauguration ceremonies, on March 4, 1881

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/GARFIELD%2C_James_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/200px-GARFIELD%2C_James_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:GARFIELD,%5FJames%5FA-President%5F%28BEP%5Fengraved%5Fportrait%29.jpg)

Line engraving of Garfield, produced around 1902 by the Bureau of Engraving and Printing as part of a presentation album of the first 26 presidents

Before his inauguration, Garfield was occupied with assembling a cabinet that might engender peace between the party's Conkling and Blaine factions. Blaine's delegates had provided much of the support for Garfield's nomination, so the Maine senator received the place of honor as Secretary of State.[\[167\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978519–521-173) Blaine was not only the president's closest advisor, but he was also obsessed with knowing all that took place in the White House, and allegedly posted spies there in his absence.[\[168\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978554–555-174) Garfield nominated [William Windom](/wiki/William%5FWindom "William Windom") of Minnesota as Secretary of the Treasury, [William H. Hunt](/wiki/William%5FH.%5FHunt "William H. Hunt") of Louisiana as Secretary of the Navy, [Robert Todd Lincoln](/wiki/Robert%5FTodd%5FLincoln "Robert Todd Lincoln") as Secretary of War, and [Samuel J. Kirkwood](/wiki/Samuel%5FJ.%5FKirkwood "Samuel J. Kirkwood") of Iowa as Secretary of the Interior. New York was represented by [Thomas Lemuel James](/wiki/Thomas%5FLemuel%5FJames "Thomas Lemuel James") as Postmaster General. Garfield appointed Pennsylvania's [Wayne MacVeagh](/wiki/Wayne%5FMacVeagh "Wayne MacVeagh"), an adversary of Blaine's, as [Attorney General](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FAttorney%5FGeneral "United States Attorney General").[\[169\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198133–36-175) Blaine tried to sabotage the appointment by convincing Garfield to name an opponent of MacVeagh, [William E. Chandler](/wiki/William%5FE.%5FChandler "William E. Chandler"), as [Solicitor General](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FSolicitor%5FGeneral "United States Solicitor General") under MacVeagh. Only Chandler's rejection by the Senate forestalled MacVeagh's resignation over the matter.[\[170\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978555–561-176) 

Because Garfield was distracted by cabinet maneuvering, his inaugural address was a "compendium of platitudes" and fell below expectations.[\[171\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965330-177)[\[172\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEWeisberger2002272-178) At one high point, however, Garfield emphasized the civil rights of [African-Americans](/wiki/African-Americans "African-Americans"), saying "Freedom can never yield its fullness of blessings so long as the law or its administration places the smallest obstacle in the pathway of any virtuous citizen."[\[173\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978538–540-179) After discussing the gold standard, the need for education, and an unexpected denunciation of [Mormon polygamy](/wiki/Mormon%5Fpolygamy "Mormon polygamy"), the speech ended. The crowd applauded, but the speech, according to Peskin, "however sincerely intended, betrayed its hasty composition by the flatness of its tone and the conventionality of its subject matter."[\[174\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978539-180) 

Garfield's appointment of James infuriated Conkling, a factional opponent of the Postmaster General, who demanded a compensatory appointment for his faction, such as the position of Secretary of the Treasury. The resulting squabble occupied much of Garfield's brief presidency. The feud with Conkling reached a climax when the president, at Blaine's instigation, nominated Conkling's enemy, Judge [William H. Robertson](/wiki/William%5FH.%5FRobertson "William H. Robertson"), to be Collector of the Port of New York. This was one of the prize patronage positions below cabinet level and was then held by [Edwin A. Merritt](/wiki/Edwin%5FAtkins%5FMerritt "Edwin Atkins Merritt"). Conkling raised the time-honored principle of [senatorial courtesy](/wiki/Senatorial%5Fcourtesy "Senatorial courtesy") in an attempt to defeat the nomination, to no avail. Garfield, who believed the practice was corrupt, would not back down and threatened to withdraw all nominations unless Robertson was confirmed, intending to "settle the question whether the president is registering clerk of the Senate or the Executive of the United States."[\[175\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198138-181) Ultimately, Conkling and his New York colleague, Senator [Thomas C. Platt](/wiki/Thomas%5FC.%5FPlatt "Thomas C. Platt"), resigned their Senate seats to seek vindication but found only further humiliation when the New York legislature elected others in their places. Robertson was confirmed as Collector and Garfield's victory was clear. To Blaine's chagrin, the victorious Garfield returned to his goal of balancing the interests of party factions and nominated a number of Conkling's Stalwart friends to offices.[\[176\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978273-182) 

With his cabinet complete, Garfield had to contend with myriad office seekers. He exclaimed, "My God! What is there in this place that a man should ever get into it." Garfield's family happily settled into the White House, but he found presidential duties exasperating.[\[172\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEWeisberger2002272-178) 

### Refinance of national debt

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Refinance of national debt")\]

Garfield ordered the Secretary of the Treasury [William Windom](/wiki/William%5FWindom "William Windom") to refund (_refinance_) the national debt by calling in outstanding U.S. bonds paying 6% interest. Holders would have the option of accepting cash or new bonds at 3%, closer to the interest rates of the time. Taxpayers were saved an estimated $10 million. By comparison, federal expenditures in 1881 were below $261 million (\~$7.09 billion in 2023).[\[177\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEWeisberger2002273-183)[\[178\]](#cite%5Fnote-184) 

### Supreme Court nomination

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Supreme Court nomination")\]

In 1880, President Hayes had nominated [Stanley Matthews](/wiki/Stanley%5FMatthews%5F%28judge%29 "Stanley Matthews (judge)") to the Supreme Court but the Senate declined to act on the nomination. In March 1881, Garfield re-nominated Matthews to the Court and the Senate confirmed Matthews by a vote of 24–23.[\[179\]](#cite%5Fnote-185) According to _The New York Times_, "opposition to Matthews's Supreme Court appointment ... stemmed from his prosecution in 1859 of a newspaper editor who had assisted two runaway slaves." Because Matthews was "a professed abolitionist at the time, the matter was later framed as political expediency triumphing over moral principle."[\[180\]](#cite%5Fnote-nytimes.com-186) Matthews served on the Court until his death in 1889.[\[180\]](#cite%5Fnote-nytimes.com-186) 

### Reforms

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Reforms")\]

Main article: [Star Route scandal](/wiki/Star%5FRoute%5Fscandal "Star Route scandal")

[![A cartoon. Garfield, in night clothes and slippers, is on the doorstep looking at an ugly crying baby in a basket on the ground.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Left_Puck.jpg/200px-Left_Puck.jpg)](/wiki/File:Left%5FPuck.jpg)

An 1881 _[Puck](/wiki/Puck%5F%28magazine%29 "Puck (magazine)")_ cartoon shows Garfield finding a baby at his front door with a tag marked "Civil Service Reform, compliments of [R.B. Hayes](/wiki/Rutherford%5FB.%5FHayes "Rutherford B. Hayes")". Hayes, his predecessor in the presidency, is seen in the background dressed like a woman and holding a bag marked "R.B. Hayes' Savings, [Fremont, Ohio](/wiki/Fremont,%5FOhio "Fremont, Ohio")".

Grant and Hayes had both advocated civil service reform, and by 1881 such reform associations had organized with renewed energy across the nation. Garfield sympathized with them, believing the [spoils system](/wiki/Spoils%5Fsystem "Spoils system") damaged the presidency and often eclipsed more important concerns.[\[181\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198139–41-187) Some reformers became disappointed when Garfield promoted limited tenure only to minor office seekers and gave appointments to his old friends.[\[181\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198139–41-187) 

Corruption in the post office also cried out for reform. In April 1880, there had been a congressional investigation of corruption in the [Post Office Department](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FPost%5FOffice%5FDepartment "United States Post Office Department"), where profiteering rings allegedly stole millions of dollars, securing bogus mail contracts on [star routes](/wiki/Star%5Froutes "Star routes").[\[182\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978578-188) After obtaining contracts with the lowest bid, costs to run the mail routes would be escalated and profits would be divided among ring members. Shortly after taking office, Garfield received word of postal corruption by an alleged star route ringleader, Assistant Postmaster General [Thomas J. Brady](/wiki/Thomas%5FJ.%5FBrady "Thomas J. Brady").[\[183\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978580-189) Garfield demanded Brady's resignation and ordered prosecutions that ended in trials for conspiracy. When told that his party, including his campaign manager, [Stephen W. Dorsey](/wiki/Stephen%5FW.%5FDorsey "Stephen W. Dorsey"), was involved, Garfield directed that the corruption in the Post Office be rooted out "to the bone", regardless of where it might lead.[\[182\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978578-188) Brady resigned and was indicted for conspiracy, though jury trials in 1882 and 1883 found Brady not guilty.[\[184\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198194–95-190) 

### Civil rights and education

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Civil rights and education")\]

[![Formal seated portrait in oils](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Jgarfield.jpeg/200px-Jgarfield.jpeg)](/wiki/File:Jgarfield.jpeg)

Official White House portrait of James Garfield, 1881

Garfield believed the key to improving the state of African American civil rights was government supported education.[\[185\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198148-191) During Reconstruction, [freedmen](/wiki/Freedman "Freedman") had gained citizenship and suffrage, which enabled them to participate in government, but Garfield believed their rights were being eroded by Southern white resistance and illiteracy, and he was concerned that blacks would become America's permanent "[peasantry](/wiki/Peasantry "Peasantry")".[\[186\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198148–49-192) He proposed a "universal" education system funded by the federal government. In February 1866, as a congressman from Ohio, Garfield and Ohio School Commissioner Emerson Edward White had drafted a bill for the National Department of Education. They believed that through the use of statistics they could push the US Congress to establish a federal agency for school reform.[\[187\]](#cite%5Fnote-193) But by the time of Garfield's presidency, Congress and the northern white public had lost interest in African-American rights, and Congress did not pass federal funding for universal education during his term.[\[186\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198148–49-192) Garfield also worked to appoint several African Americans to prominent positions: [Frederick Douglass](/wiki/Frederick%5FDouglass "Frederick Douglass"), recorder of deeds in Washington; [Robert Elliot](/wiki/Robert%5FB.%5FElliott "Robert B. Elliott"), special agent to the Treasury; [John M. Langston](/wiki/John%5FM.%5FLangston "John M. Langston"), [Haitian](/wiki/Haiti "Haiti") minister; and [Blanche K. Bruce](/wiki/Blanche%5FBruce "Blanche Bruce"), register to the Treasury. Garfield believed Southern support for the Republican Party could be gained by "commercial and industrial" interests rather than race issues and began to reverse Hayes's policy of conciliating Southern Democrats.[\[188\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198149–50-194) He appointed [William H. Hunt](/wiki/William%5FH.%5FHunt "William H. Hunt"), a Republican from Louisiana, as Secretary of the Navy.[\[188\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198149–50-194) To break the hold of the resurgent Democratic Party in the Solid South, Garfield took patronage advice from Virginia Senator [William Mahone](/wiki/William%5FMahone "William Mahone") of the biracial independent [Readjuster Party](/wiki/Readjuster%5FParty "Readjuster Party"), hoping to add the independents' strength to the Republicans' there.[\[189\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke198150–53-195) 

### Foreign policy and naval reform

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Foreign policy and naval reform")\]

Further information: [History of U.S. foreign policy, 1861–1897](/wiki/History%5Fof%5FU.S.%5Fforeign%5Fpolicy,%5F1861%E2%80%931897 "History of U.S. foreign policy, 1861–1897")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/James_G._Blaine_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/170px-James_G._Blaine_-_Brady-Handy.jpg)](/wiki/File:James%5FG.%5FBlaine%5F-%5FBrady-Handy.jpg)

[James G. Blaine](/wiki/James%5FG.%5FBlaine "James G. Blaine"), Garfield's Secretary of State

Garfield had little foreign policy experience, so he leaned heavily on Blaine.[\[190\]](#cite%5Fnote-196) They agreed on the need to promote freer trade, especially within the [Western Hemisphere](/wiki/Western%5FHemisphere "Western Hemisphere").[\[191\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200062–64-197) Garfield and Blaine believed increasing trade with [Latin America](/wiki/Latin%5FAmerica "Latin America") would be the best way to keep the [United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland](/wiki/United%5FKingdom%5Fof%5FGreat%5FBritain%5Fand%5FIreland "United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland") from dominating the region.[\[191\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200062–64-197) And by encouraging exports, they believed they could increase American prosperity.[\[191\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200062–64-197) Garfield authorized Blaine to call for a Pan-American conference in 1882 to mediate disputes among the Latin American nations and to serve as a forum for talks on increasing trade.[\[192\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200065–66Doenecke198155–57-198) 

At the same time, they hoped to negotiate a peace in the [War of the Pacific](/wiki/War%5Fof%5Fthe%5FPacific "War of the Pacific") then being fought by [Bolivia](/wiki/Bolivia "Bolivia"), [Chile](/wiki/Chile "Chile"), and [Peru](/wiki/Peru "Peru").[\[192\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200065–66Doenecke198155–57-198) Blaine favored a resolution that would result in Peru yielding no territory, but Chile by 1881 had occupied the Peruvian capital of [Lima](/wiki/Lima "Lima"), and rejected any settlement that restored the previous _status quo_.[\[193\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200070Doenecke198157–58-199) 

Garfield sought to expand American influence in other areas, calling for renegotiation of the [Clayton–Bulwer Treaty](/wiki/Clayton%E2%80%93Bulwer%5FTreaty "Clayton–Bulwer Treaty") to allow the United States to construct [a canal through Panama](/wiki/Panama%5FCanal "Panama Canal") without British involvement and attempting to reduce British influence in the strategically located [Kingdom of Hawaii](/wiki/Kingdom%5Fof%5FHawaii "Kingdom of Hawaii").[\[194\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200074–80Peskin1978576–577-200) Garfield's and Blaine's plans for the United States' involvement in the world stretched even beyond the Western Hemisphere, as he sought commercial treaties with [Korea](/wiki/Joseon%5FDynasty "Joseon Dynasty") and [Madagascar](/wiki/Merina%5FKingdom "Merina Kingdom").[\[195\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECrapol200081Doenecke198171–73-201) Garfield also considered enhancing U.S. military strength abroad, asking Navy Secretary Hunt to investigate the navy's condition with an eye toward expansion and modernization.[\[196\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981145–147-202) In the end, these ambitious plans came to nothing after Garfield was assassinated. Nine countries had accepted invitations to the Pan-American conference, but the invitations were withdrawn in April 1882 after Blaine resigned from the cabinet and Arthur, Garfield's successor, cancelled the conference.[\[197\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981130–131-203)[\[g\]](#cite%5Fnote-204) Naval reform continued under Arthur, on a more modest scale than Garfield and Hunt had envisioned, ultimately ending in the construction of the [Squadron of Evolution](/wiki/Squadron%5Fof%5FEvolution "Squadron of Evolution").[\[198\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981148–149-205) 

## Assassination

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Assassination")\]

### Guiteau and shooting

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Guiteau and shooting")\]

Main article: [Assassination of James A. Garfield](/wiki/Assassination%5Fof%5FJames%5FA.%5FGarfield "Assassination of James A. Garfield")

[Charles J. Guiteau](/wiki/Charles%5FJ.%5FGuiteau "Charles J. Guiteau") had followed various professions in his life, but in 1880 had determined to gain federal office by supporting what he expected would be the winning Republican ticket.[\[199\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003114-206) He composed a speech, "Garfield vs. Hancock", and got it printed by the Republican National Committee. One means of persuading the voters in that era was through orators expounding on the candidate's merits, but with the Republicans seeking more famous men, Guiteau received few opportunities to speak.[\[200\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003151–152-207) On one occasion, according to Kenneth D. Ackerman, Guiteau was unable to finish his speech due to nerves. Guiteau, who considered himself a [Stalwart](/wiki/Stalwart%5F%28politics%29 "Stalwart (politics)"), deemed his contribution to Garfield's victory sufficient to justify his appointment to the position of consul in Paris, despite the fact that he spoke no French, nor any foreign language.[\[201\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003189–190,%5F237-208) One medical expert has since described Guiteau as possibly a narcissistic schizophrenic;[\[202\]](#cite%5Fnote-209) neuroscientist [Kent Kiehl](/wiki/Kent%5FKiehl "Kent Kiehl") assessed him as a clinical psychopath.[\[203\]](#cite%5Fnote-210) 

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg/200px-Garfield_assassination_engraving_cropped.jpg)](/wiki/File:Garfield%5Fassassination%5Fengraving%5Fcropped.jpg)

Garfield, shot by [Charles J. Guiteau](/wiki/Charles%5FJ.%5FGuiteau "Charles J. Guiteau"), collapses as Secretary of State Blaine gestures for help. Engraving from _[Frank Leslie's Illustrated Newspaper](/wiki/Frank%5FLeslie%27s%5FIllustrated%5FNewspaper "Frank Leslie's Illustrated Newspaper")_.

One of Garfield's more wearying duties was seeing office-seekers, and he saw Guiteau at least once. White House officials suggested to Guiteau that he approach Blaine, as the consulship was within the Department of State.[\[204\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003237–238-211) Blaine also saw the public regularly, and Guiteau became a regular at these sessions. Blaine, who had no intention of giving Guiteau a position he was unqualified for and had not earned, simply said the deadlock in the Senate over Robertson's nomination made it impossible to consider the Paris consulship, which required Senate confirmation.[\[205\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003278–279-212) Once the New York senators had resigned, and Robertson had been confirmed as Collector, Guiteau pressed his claim, and Blaine told him he would not receive the position.[\[206\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003299-213) 

Guiteau came to believe he had lost the position because he was a Stalwart. He decided the only way to end the Republican Party's internecine warfare was for Garfield to die—though he had nothing personal against the president. Arthur's succession would restore peace, he felt, and lead to rewards for fellow Stalwarts, including Guiteau.[\[207\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003305–308-214) 

The [assassination of Abraham Lincoln](/wiki/Assassination%5Fof%5FAbraham%5FLincoln "Assassination of Abraham Lincoln") was deemed a fluke due to the Civil War, and Garfield, like most people, saw no reason the president should be guarded; his movements and plans were often printed in the newspapers. Guiteau knew Garfield would leave Washington for a cooler climate on July 2, 1881, and made plans to kill him before then. He purchased a gun he thought would look good in a museum, and followed Garfield several times, but each time his plans were frustrated, or he lost his nerve.[\[208\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003313–316-215) His opportunities dwindled to one—Garfield's departure by train for New Jersey on the morning of July 2.[\[209\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003328–330-216) 

Guiteau concealed himself by the ladies' waiting room at the [Sixth Street Station](/wiki/Baltimore%5Fand%5FPotomac%5FRailroad%5FStation "Baltimore and Potomac Railroad Station") of the [Baltimore and Potomac Railroad](/wiki/Baltimore%5Fand%5FPotomac%5FRailroad "Baltimore and Potomac Railroad"), from where Garfield was scheduled to depart. Most of Garfield's cabinet planned to accompany him at least part of the way. Blaine, who was to remain in Washington, came to the station to see him off. The two men were deep in conversation and did not notice Guiteau before he took out his revolver and shot Garfield twice, once in the back and once in the arm. Guiteau attempted to leave the station but was quickly captured.[\[210\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978596-217) As Blaine recognized him, Guiteau was led away, and said, "I did it. I will go to jail for it. I am a Stalwart and Arthur will be President."[\[h\]](#cite%5Fnote-218)[\[211\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335-219) News of his motivation to benefit the Stalwarts reached many with the news of the shooting, causing rage against that faction.[\[212\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335–340-220) 

### Treatment and death

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Treatment and death")\]

[![An ornate Victorian Gothic style building with a square tower](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/B%26PStation1908.jpg/220px-B%26PStation1908.jpg)](/wiki/File:B%26PStation1908.jpg)

[Baltimore and Potomac Railroad Station](/wiki/Baltimore%5Fand%5FPotomac%5FRailroad%5FStation "Baltimore and Potomac Railroad Station") in Washington D.C., where Garfield was shot July 2, 1881

Garfield was struck by two shots: one glanced off his arm while the other pierced his back, shattering a rib and embedding itself in his abdomen. "My God, what is this?" he exclaimed.[\[213\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003333–334-221) Among those at the station was [Robert Todd Lincoln](/wiki/Robert%5FTodd%5FLincoln "Robert Todd Lincoln"), who was deeply upset, thinking back to when his father Abraham Lincoln was assassinated 16 years earlier. Garfield was taken on a mattress upstairs to a private office, where several doctors examined him. At his request, Garfield was taken back to the White House, and his wife, then in New Jersey, was sent for.[\[214\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003335–336-222) Blaine sent word to Vice President Arthur in New York City, who received threats against his life because of his animosity toward Garfield and Guiteau's statements.[\[215\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTERutkow200688–89-223) 

Although [Joseph Lister](/wiki/Joseph%5FLister,%5F1st%5FBaron%5FLister "Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister")'s pioneering work in antisepsis was known to American doctors, few of them had confidence in it, and none of his advocates were among Garfield's treating physicians.[\[216\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTERutkow2006106–107-224) The physician who took charge at the depot and then at the White House was [Doctor Willard Bliss](/wiki/Doctor%5FWillard%5FBliss "Doctor Willard Bliss").[\[i\]](#cite%5Fnote-225) A noted physician and surgeon, Bliss was an old friend of Garfield, and about a dozen doctors, led by Bliss, were soon probing the wound with unsterilized fingers and instruments. Garfield was given [morphine](/wiki/Morphine "Morphine") for the pain, and asked Bliss to frankly tell him his chances, which Bliss put at one in a hundred. "Well, Doctor, we'll take that chance."[\[217\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003346-226) 

Over the next few days, Garfield made some improvement, as the nation viewed the news from the capital and prayed. Although he never stood again, he was able to sit up and write several times, and his recovery was viewed so positively that a steamer was fitted out as a seagoing hospital to aid with his convalescence. He was nourished on oatmeal [porridge](/wiki/Porridge "Porridge") (which he detested) and milk from a cow on the White House lawn. When told that Indian chief [Sitting Bull](/wiki/Sitting%5FBull "Sitting Bull"), a prisoner of the army, was starving, Garfield said, "Let him starve..." initially, but a few moments later said, "No, send him my oatmeal."[\[218\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978601–602-227) 

[X-ray imaging](/wiki/Projectional%5Fradiography "Projectional radiography"), which could have assisted physicians in precisely locating the bullet in Garfield's body, would not be invented for another 14 years. [Alexander Graham Bell](/wiki/Alexander%5FGraham%5FBell "Alexander Graham Bell") tried to locate the bullet with a primitive metal detector, but was unsuccessful, though the device had been effective when tested on others. But Bliss limited its use on Garfield, ensuring he remained in charge. Because Bliss insisted the bullet rested someplace it did not, the detector could not locate it. Bell shortly returned after adjusting his device, which emitted an unusual tone in the area where Bliss believed the bullet was lodged. Bliss took this as confirmation that the bullet was where he declared it to be. Bliss recorded the test as a success, saying it was: 

> now unanimously agreed that the location of the ball has been ascertained with reasonable certainty, and that it lies, as heretofore stated, in the front wall of the abdomen, immediately over the groin, about five inches \[130 mm\] below and to the right of the navel.[\[219\]](#cite%5Fnote-228)

One means of keeping Garfield comfortable in Washington's summer heat was one of the first successful [air conditioning](/wiki/Air%5Fconditioning "Air conditioning") units: air propelled by fans over ice and then dried reduced the temperature in the sickroom by 20 °F (11 °C).[\[218\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978601–602-227) Engineers from the navy, and other scientists, worked together to develop it, though there were problems to solve, such as excessive noise and increased humidity.[\[220\]](#cite%5Fnote-229) 

On July 23, Garfield took a turn for the worse when his temperature increased to 104 °F (40 °C); doctors, concerned by an [abscess](/wiki/Abscess "Abscess") at the wound, inserted a drainage tube. This initially helped, and the bedridden Garfield held a brief cabinet meeting on July 29; members were under orders from Bliss to discuss nothing that might excite Garfield.[\[221\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978603-230) Doctors probed the abscess, hoping to find the bullet; they likely made the infections worse. Garfield performed only one official act in August, signing an extradition paper. By the end of the month, he was much feebler than he had been, and his weight had decreased from 210 pounds (95 kg) to 130 pounds (59 kg).[\[222\]](#cite%5Fnote-231)[\[223\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003374–375-232) 

Garfield had long been anxious to escape hot, unhealthy Washington, and in early September the doctors agreed to move him to [Elberon](/wiki/Elberon,%5FNew%5FJersey "Elberon, New Jersey"), part of [Long Branch, New Jersey](/wiki/Long%5FBranch,%5FNew%5FJersey "Long Branch, New Jersey"), where his wife had recovered earlier in the summer. He left the White House for the last time on September 5, traveling in a specially cushioned railway car; a spur line to the [Francklyn Cottage](/wiki/Charles%5FG.%5FFrancklyn "Charles G. Francklyn"), a seaside mansion given over to his use, was built in a night by volunteers. After arriving in Elberon the next day, Garfield was moved from the train car to a bedroom where he could see the ocean as officials and reporters maintained what became (after an initial rally) a death watch. Garfield's personal secretary, [Joe Stanley Brown](/wiki/Joe%5FStanley%5FBrown "Joe Stanley Brown"), wrote forty years later, "to this day I cannot hear the sound of the low slow roll of the Atlantic on the shore, the sound which filled my ears as I walked from my cottage to his bedside, without recalling again that ghastly tragedy."[\[224\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003374–376-233) 

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chester_A._Arthur_by_Abraham_Bogardus.jpg/170px-Chester_A._Arthur_by_Abraham_Bogardus.jpg)](/wiki/File:Chester%5FA.%5FArthur%5Fby%5FAbraham%5FBogardus.jpg)

Vice-president [Chester A. Arthur](/wiki/Chester%5FA.%5FArthur "Chester A. Arthur") assumed the presidency after Garfield's death.

On September 18, Garfield asked Colonel A.F. Rockwell, a friend, if he would have a place in history. Rockwell assured him he would and told Garfield he had much work still before him. But his response was, "No, my work is done."[\[225\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965355-234) The following day, Garfield, then suffering also from pneumonia and hypertension, marveled that he could not pick up a glass despite feeling well and went to sleep without discomfort. He awoke that evening around 10:15 p.m. complaining of great pain in his chest to his chief of staff General [David Swaim](/wiki/David%5FG.%5FSwaim "David G. Swaim"), who was watching him, as he placed his hand over his heart.[\[226\]](#cite%5Fnote-235) The president then requested a drink of water from Swaim. After finishing his glass, Garfield said, "Oh Swaim, this terrible pain—press your hand on it." As Swaim put his hand on Garfield's chest, Garfield's hands went up reflexively. Clutching his heart, he exclaimed, "Oh, Swaim, can't you stop this? Oh, oh, Swaim!" Those were Garfield's last words.[\[227\]](#cite%5Fnote-236) Swaim ordered another attendant to send for Bliss, who found Garfield unconscious. Despite efforts to revive him, Garfield never awoke, and he was pronounced dead at about 10:30 p.m.[\[228\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003376–377Rutkow2006127-237)[\[229\]](#cite%5Fnote-238) Learning from a reporter of Garfield's death the following day, Chester A. Arthur took the presidential oath of office administered by New York Supreme Court Justice [John R. Brady](/wiki/John%5FR.%5FBrady "John R. Brady").[\[230\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608-239) 

According to some historians and medical experts, Garfield might have survived his wounds had the doctors attending him had at their disposal today's medical research, knowledge, techniques, and equipment.[\[231\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-240)[\[232\]](#cite%5Fnote-241)[\[233\]](#cite%5Fnote-242) Standard medical practice at the time dictated that priority be given to locating the path of the bullet. Several of his doctors inserted their [unsterilized](/wiki/Asepsis "Asepsis") fingers into the wound to probe for the bullet, a common practice in the 1880s.[\[231\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-240) Historians agree that massive infection was a significant factor in Garfield's demise.[\[231\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-240) Biographer Peskin said medical malpractice did not contribute to Garfield's death; the inevitable infection and blood poisoning that would ensue from a deep bullet wound resulted in damage to multiple organs and spinal fragmentation.[\[234\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978607-243) Rutkow, a professor of surgery at the [University of Medicine and Dentistry of New Jersey](/wiki/University%5Fof%5FMedicine%5Fand%5FDentistry%5Fof%5FNew%5FJersey "University of Medicine and Dentistry of New Jersey"), has argued that starvation also played a role. Rutkow suggests "Garfield had such a nonlethal wound. In today's world, he would have gone home in a matter of two or three days."[\[231\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTESchaffer2006-240) The conventional narrative regarding Garfield's post-shooting medical condition was challenged by Theodore Pappas and Shahrzad Joharifard in a 2013 article in _[The American Journal of Surgery](/wiki/The%5FAmerican%5FJournal%5Fof%5FSurgery "The American Journal of Surgery")_. They argued that Garfield died from a late rupture of a splenic artery [pseudoaneurysm](/wiki/Pseudoaneurysm "Pseudoaneurysm"), which developed secondary to the path of the bullet adjacent to the splenic artery. They also argued that his sepsis was actually caused by post-traumatic acute acalculous [cholecystitis](/wiki/Cholecystitis "Cholecystitis"). Based on the [autopsy](/wiki/Autopsy "Autopsy") report, the authors speculate that his gallbladder subsequently ruptured, leading to the development of a large bile-containing abscess adjacent to the gallbladder. Pappas and Joharifard say this caused the septic decline in Garfield's condition that was visible starting from July 23, 1881\. Pappas and Joharifard also state that they don't believe that Garfield's doctors could have saved him even if they had been aware of his cholecystitis, since the first successful [cholecystectomy](/wiki/Cholecystectomy "Cholecystectomy") ([surgical](/wiki/Surgical "Surgical") removal of the gallbladder) was performed a year after Garfield's death.[\[235\]](#cite%5Fnote-244) 

Guiteau was indicted on October 14, 1881, for the murder of the president. During his trial, Guiteau declared that he was not responsible for Garfield's death, admitting to the shooting but not the killing. In his defense, Guiteau wrote: "General Garfield died from malpractice. According to his own physicians, he was not fatally shot. The doctors who mistreated him ought to bear the odium of his death, and not his assailant. They ought to be indicted for murdering James A. Garfield, and not me."[\[236\]](#cite%5Fnote-245) After a chaotic trial in which Guiteau often interrupted and argued, and in which his counsel used the [insanity defense](/wiki/Insanity%5Fdefense "Insanity defense"), the jury found him guilty on January 25, 1882, and he was sentenced to death by hanging. Guiteau may have had [neurosyphilis](/wiki/Neurosyphilis "Neurosyphilis"), a disease that causes physiological mental impairment.[\[237\]](#cite%5Fnote-246) He was executed on June 30, 1882.[\[238\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003444–446-247) 

## Funeral, memorials and commemorations

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Funeral, memorials and commemorations")\]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Garfield-casket.jpg/200px-Garfield-casket.jpg)](/wiki/File:Garfield-casket.jpg)

Garfield's casket [lying in state](/wiki/Lying%5Fin%5Fstate "Lying in state") at the [Capitol Rotunda](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FCapitol%5Frotunda "United States Capitol rotunda")

Garfield's funeral train left Long Branch on the same special track that had brought him there, traveling over tracks blanketed with flowers and past houses adorned with flags. His body was transported to the Capitol and then continued on to Cleveland for burial.[\[239\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608–609-248) Shocked by his death, [Marine Band](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FMarine%5FBand "United States Marine Band") leader [John Philip Sousa](/wiki/John%5FPhilip%5FSousa "John Philip Sousa") composed the march "[In Memoriam](/wiki/In%5FMemoriam:%5FPresident%5FGarfield%27s%5FFuneral%5FMarch "In Memoriam: President Garfield's Funeral March")", which was played when Garfield's body was received in [Washington, D.C.](/wiki/Washington,%5FD.C. "Washington, D.C.")[\[240\]](#cite%5Fnote-249) More than 70,000 citizens, some waiting over three hours, passed by Garfield's coffin as his body [lay in state](/wiki/Lying%5Fin%5Fstate "Lying in state") from September 21 to 23, 1881,[\[241\]](#cite%5Fnote-250) at the [United States Capitol rotunda](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FCapitol%5Frotunda "United States Capitol rotunda"); on September 25, in Cleveland, Garfield's casket was paraded down [Euclid Avenue](/wiki/Euclid%5FAvenue%5F%28Cleveland%29 "Euclid Avenue (Cleveland)") from Wilson Avenue to [Public Square](/wiki/Public%5FSquare,%5FCleveland "Public Square, Cleveland"), with those in attendance including former presidents Grant and Hayes, and Generals [William Sherman](/wiki/William%5FTecumseh%5FSherman "William Tecumseh Sherman"), [Sheridan](/wiki/Philip%5FSheridan "Philip Sheridan") and Hancock.[\[242\]](#cite%5Fnote-251) More than 150,000—a number equal to the city's population—likewise paid their respects, and Sousa's march was again played.[\[239\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608–609-248)[\[243\]](#cite%5Fnote-252) Garfield's body was temporarily interred in the [Schofield](/wiki/Levi%5FScofield "Levi Scofield") family vault in Cleveland's [Lake View Cemetery](/wiki/Lake%5FView%5FCemetery "Lake View Cemetery") until his permanent memorial was built.[\[239\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978608–609-248)[\[244\]](#cite%5Fnote-253) 

Memorials to Garfield were erected across the country. On April 10, 1882, seven months after Garfield's death, the U.S. Post Office Department issued a postage stamp in his honor.[\[245\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTESmithsonian%5F2006-254) In 1884, sculptor [Frank Happersberger](/wiki/Frank%5FHappersberger "Frank Happersberger") completed a monument on the grounds of the [San Francisco Conservatory of Flowers](/wiki/Conservatory%5Fof%5FFlowers "Conservatory of Flowers").[\[246\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTERadfordRadford200223-255) In 1887, the [James A. Garfield Monument](/wiki/James%5FA.%5FGarfield%5FMonument "James A. Garfield Monument") was dedicated in Washington.[\[247\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003441-256) [Another monument](/wiki/James%5FGarfield%5FMemorial,%5FPhiladelphia "James Garfield Memorial, Philadelphia"), in Philadelphia's [Fairmount Park](/wiki/Fairmount%5FPark "Fairmount Park"), was erected in 1896.[\[248\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBach1992207-257) In [Victoria, Australia](/wiki/Victoria,%5FAustralia "Victoria, Australia"), [Cannibal Creek](/wiki/Garfield,%5FVictoria "Garfield, Victoria") was renamed Garfield in his honor.[\[249\]](#cite%5Fnote-258) 

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/The_Garfield_Obsequies_at_Cleveland%2C_Schofield_vault%2C_temporary_resting_place_of_the_remains%2C_from_Robert_N._Dennis_collection_of_stereoscopic_views.jpg/200px-The_Garfield_Obsequies_at_Cleveland%2C_Schofield_vault%2C_temporary_resting_place_of_the_remains%2C_from_Robert_N._Dennis_collection_of_stereoscopic_views.jpg)](/wiki/File:The%5FGarfield%5FObsequies%5Fat%5FCleveland,%5FSchofield%5Fvault,%5Ftemporary%5Fresting%5Fplace%5Fof%5Fthe%5Fremains,%5Ffrom%5FRobert%5FN.%5FDennis%5Fcollection%5Fof%5Fstereoscopic%5Fviews.jpg)

[Stereoscopic images](/wiki/Stereoscopy "Stereoscopy") of the [Schofield](/wiki/Levi%5FScofield "Levi Scofield") family vault where Garfield's remains were kept until completion of his permanent memorial[\[250\]](#cite%5Fnote-259)

On May 19, 1890, Garfield's body was permanently interred, with great solemnity and fanfare, in a [mausoleum](/wiki/James%5FA.%5FGarfield%5FMemorial "James A. Garfield Memorial") in Lake View Cemetery. Attending the dedication ceremonies were former President Hayes, President Benjamin Harrison, and future president [William McKinley](/wiki/William%5FMcKinley "William McKinley").[\[251\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEMemorial%5F189046–49-260) Garfield's Treasury Secretary, William Windom, also attended.[\[251\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEMemorial%5F189046–49-260) Harrison said Garfield was always a "student and instructor" and that his life works and death would "continue to be instructive and inspiring incidents in American history".[\[252\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEMemorial%5F189051-261) Three panels on the monument display Garfield as a teacher, Union major general, and [orator](/wiki/Orator "Orator"); another shows him taking the presidential oath, and a fifth shows his body lying in state at the Capitol rotunda in Washington, D.C.[\[253\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEMemorial%5F189034–35-262) 

Garfield's murder by a deranged office-seeker awakened public awareness of the need for civil service reform legislation. Senator [George H. Pendleton](/wiki/George%5FH.%5FPendleton "George H. Pendleton"), a Democrat from Ohio, launched a reform effort that resulted in the [Pendleton Act](/wiki/Pendleton%5FAct "Pendleton Act") in January 1883.[\[254\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981100–102-263) This act reversed the "spoils system" where office seekers paid up or gave political service to obtain or keep federally appointed positions.[\[254\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981100–102-263) Under the act, appointments were awarded on merit and competitive examination.[\[255\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981102–103-264) To ensure the reform was implemented, Congress and Arthur established and funded the [Civil Service Commission](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FCivil%5FService%5FCommission "United States Civil Service Commission"). The Pendleton Act, however, covered only 10% of federal government workers.[\[255\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981102–103-264) For Arthur, previously known for having been a "veteran spoilsman", civil service reform became his most noteworthy achievement.[\[256\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978610-265) 

A [marble statue of Garfield](/wiki/Statue%5Fof%5FJames%5FA.%5FGarfield%5F%28U.S.%5FCapitol%29 "Statue of James A. Garfield (U.S. Capitol)") by [Charles Niehaus](/wiki/Charles%5FNiehaus "Charles Niehaus") was added to the [National Statuary Hall Collection](/wiki/National%5FStatuary%5FHall%5FCollection "National Statuary Hall Collection") in the [Capitol](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FCapitol "United States Capitol") in [Washington D.C.](/wiki/Washington%5FD.C. "Washington D.C."), a gift from the State of [Ohio](/wiki/Ohio "Ohio") in 1886.[\[257\]](#cite%5Fnote-266) 

Garfield is honored with a life-size bronze sculpture inside the [Cuyahoga County Soldiers' and Sailors' Monument](/wiki/Soldiers%27%5Fand%5FSailors%27%5FMonument%5F%28Cleveland%29 "Soldiers' and Sailors' Monument (Cleveland)") in Cleveland, Ohio.[\[258\]](#cite%5Fnote-267) 

On March 2, 2019, the [National Park Service](/wiki/National%5FPark%5FService "National Park Service") erected exhibit panels in Washington to mark the site of his assassination.[\[259\]](#cite%5Fnote-268)

## Legacy and historical view

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Legacy and historical view")\]

For a few years after his assassination, Garfield's life story was seen as an exemplar of the American success story—that even the poorest boy might someday become President of the United States. Peskin wrote: "In mourning Garfield, Americans were not only honoring a president; they were paying tribute to a man whose life story embodied their own most cherished aspirations."[\[260\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin2000-269) As the rivalry between Stalwarts and [Half-Breeds](/wiki/Half-Breeds%5F%28politics%29 "Half-Breeds (politics)") faded from the scene in the late 1880s and after, so too did memories of Garfield. In the 1890s, Americans became disillusioned with politicians, and looked elsewhere for inspiration, focusing on industrialists, labor leaders, scientists, and others as their heroes. Increasingly, Garfield's short time as president was forgotten.[\[261\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTERutkow2006136-270) 

| External videos                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ![video icon](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png) [_Booknotes_ interview with Kenneth Ackerman on _Dark Horse: The Surprise Election and Political Murder of President James A. Garfield_, July 27, 2003](https://www.c-span.org/video/?159447-1/dark-horse-president-james-garfield), [C-SPAN](/wiki/C-SPAN "C-SPAN") |

The 20th century saw no revival for Garfield. [Thomas Wolfe](/wiki/Thomas%5FWolfe "Thomas Wolfe") deemed the presidents of the [Gilded Age](/wiki/Gilded%5FAge "Gilded Age"), including Garfield, "lost Americans" whose "gravely vacant and bewhiskered faces mixed, melted, swam together".[\[262\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003399-271) The politicians of the Gilded Age faded from the public eye, their luster eclipsed by those who had influenced America outside of political office during that time; the [robber barons](/wiki/Robber%5Fbaron%5F%28industrialist%29 "Robber baron (industrialist)"), the inventors, those who had sought social reform, and others who had lived as America rapidly changed. Current events and more recent figures occupied America's attention. According to Ackerman, "the busy Twentieth Century has made Garfield's era seem remote and irrelevant, its leaders ridiculed for their very obscurity."[\[262\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEAckerman2003399-271) 

Garfield's biographers, and those who have studied his presidency, tend to think well of him, and that his presidency saw a promising start before its untimely end. Historian [Justus D. Doenecke](/wiki/Justus%5FD.%5FDoenecke "Justus D. Doenecke"), while deeming Garfield a bit of an enigma, chronicles his achievements: "by winning a victory over the Stalwarts, he enhanced both the power and prestige of his office. As a man, he was intelligent, sensitive, and alert, and his knowledge of how government worked was unmatched."[\[263\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981182-272) Doenecke criticizes Garfield's dismissal of Merritt in Robertson's favor, and wonders if the president was truly in command of the situation even after the latter's confirmation.[\[264\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEDoenecke1981183-273) In 1931, Caldwell wrote: "If Garfield lives in history, it will be partly on account of the charm of his personality—but also because in life and in death, he struck the first shrewd blows against a dangerous system of boss rule which seemed for a time about to engulf the politics of the nation. Perhaps if he had lived he could have done no more."[\[265\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECaldwell1965363-274) Rutkow writes that "James Abram Garfield's presidency is reduced to a tantalizing 'what if.'"[\[261\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTERutkow2006136-270) 

In 2002, historian Bernard A. Weisberger said, "\[Garfield\] was, to some extent, a perfect moderate. He read widely (and unobtrusively) without its visibly affecting his Christianity, his Republicanism, or his general laissez-faire orthodoxy. He was not so much a scholar in politics as a politic scholar."[\[266\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEWeisberger2002271-275) Peskin believes Garfield deserves more credit for his political career than he has received: "True, his accomplishments were neither bold nor heroic, but his was not an age that called for heroism. His stormy presidency was brief, and in some respects, unfortunate, but he did leave the office stronger than he found it. As a public man he had a hand in almost every issue of national importance for almost two decades, while as a party leader he, along with Blaine, forged the Republican Party into the instrument that would lead the United States into the twentieth century."[\[267\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978611-276) 

## Notes

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FA.%5FGarfield&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Notes")\]

1. **[^](#cite%5Fref-3)** Divisions in the Stone-Campbell Movement were not recognized until the 20th Century. The names Christian Church, Church of Christ, and Disciples of Christ were used interchangeably until then.[\[2\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEMcAlisterTucker1975252-2)
2. **[^](#cite%5Fref-5)** Orange Township had been in the [Western Reserve](/wiki/Western%5FReserve "Western Reserve") until 1800.
3. **[^](#cite%5Fref-54)** Biographer Allan Peskin speculated that he may have had [infectious hepatitis](/wiki/Infectious%5Fhepatitis "Infectious hepatitis") instead.[\[51\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978632–633-53)
4. **[^](#cite%5Fref-73)** Until the ratification of the Twentieth Amendment in 1933, Congress convened annually in December.
5. **[^](#cite%5Fref-96)** On June 13, 1868, the House passed the [14th Amendment](/wiki/Fourteenth%5FAmendment%5Fto%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates%5FConstitution "Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution") that gave [African Americans](/wiki/African%5FAmericans "African Americans") U.S. citizenship.[\[91\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEFoner2014253–254-95)
6. **[^](#cite%5Fref-126)** Garfield typically won two or three times his Democratic opponents' votes.[\[120\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPeskin1978148,%5F244,%5F277,%5F292-125)
7. **[^](#cite%5Fref-204)** In October 1883, the War of the Pacific was settled without American involvement, with the [Treaty of Ancón](/wiki/Treaty%5Fof%5FAnc%C3%B3n "Treaty of Ancón").
8. **[^](#cite%5Fref-218)** The words vary in some sources
9. **[^](#cite%5Fref-225)** "Doctor" was his given name.
